Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units can be arranged to create a convergent array of NH groups, which can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests. This arrangement enhances the selectivity and affinity for anions, especially those that are good hydrogen bond acceptors (such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate). Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole can contribute to the overall stability of the host structure and, in some cases, to additional non-covalent interactions (such as π-π stacking or anion-π interactions). The effectiveness of pyrrole-based hosts is exemplified by classic systems like calixpyrroles and porphyrins, which have been extensively studied for their anion binding properties. The combination of preorganized binding sites, strong hydrogen bonding, and the ability to fine-tune the host's cavity size and electronic environment makes pyrrole-containing supramolecular hosts Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units can be arranged to create a convergent array of NH groups, which can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests. This arrangement enhances the selectivity and affinity for anions, especially those that are good hydrogen bond acceptors (such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate). Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole can contribute to the overall stability of the host structure and, in some cases, to additional non-covalent interactions (such as π-π stacking or anion-π interactions). The effectiveness of pyrrole-based hosts is exemplified by classic systems like calixpyrroles and porphyrins, which have been extensively studied for their anion binding properties. The combination of preorganized binding sites, strong hydrogen bonding, and the ability to fine-tune the host's cavity size and electronic environment makes pyrrole-containing supramolecular hosts In supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units can be arranged to create a convergent array of NH groups, which can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests. This arrangement enhances the selectivity and affinity for anions, especially those that are good hydrogen bond acceptors (such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate). Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In supramolecular hosts, multiple pyrrole units can be arranged to create a convergent array of NH groups, which can form strong, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests. This arrangement enhances the selectivity and affinity for anions, especially those that are good hydrogen bond acceptors (such as chloride, sulfate, or phosphate). Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole can contribute to the overall stability of the host structure and, in some cases, to additional non-covalent interactions (such as π-π stacking or anion-π interactions). The effectiveness of pyrrole-based hosts is exemplified by classic systems like calixpyrroles and porphyrins, which have been extensively studied for their anion binding properties. The combination of preorganized binding sites, strong hydrogen bonding, and the ability to fine-tune the host's cavity size and electronic environment makes pyrrole-containing supramolecular hosts
